<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Politics_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>

<p>The <b>monarchy of the United Kingdom</b>, commonly referred to as the <b>British monarchy</b>, is the <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crown_Dependencies" title="Crown Dependencies">its dependencies</a> and its <a href="/wiki/British_Overseas_Territories" title="British Overseas Territories">overseas territories</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Monarch" title="Monarch">monarch</a>'s title is "King" (male) or "Queen" (female). The current monarch and head of state, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_II" title="Queen Elizabeth II">Queen Elizabeth II</a>, ascended the throne on the death of her father, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_George_VI" title="King George VI">King George VI</a>, on 6 February 1952.</p>
<p>The monarch and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Royal_Family" title="British Royal Family">his or her immediate family</a> undertake various official, ceremonial, diplomatic and representational duties. As the monarchy is constitutional, the monarch is limited to non-partisan functions such as bestowing <a href="/wiki/Orders,_decorations,_and_medals_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Orders, decorations, and medals of the United Kingdom">honours</a> and appointing the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister</a>. The monarch is, by tradition, <a href="/wiki/Commander-in-chief_of_the_British_Armed_Forces" title="Commander-in-chief of the British Armed Forces">commander-in-chief of the British Armed Forces</a>. Though the ultimate formal executive authority over the government of the United Kingdom is still by and through the monarch's <a href="/wiki/Royal_prerogative" title="Royal prerogative">royal prerogative</a>, these powers may only be used according to laws enacted in Parliament and, in practice, within the constraints of convention and <a href="/wiki/Precedent" title="Precedent">precedent</a>.</p>
<p>The British monarchy traces its origins from the <a href="/wiki/Petty_kingdom" title="Petty kingdom">petty kingdoms</a> of <a href="/wiki/Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages" title="Scotland in the Early Middle Ages">early medieval Scotland</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_England" title="Anglo-Saxon England">Anglo-Saxon England</a>, which consolidated into the kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Scotland</a> by the 10th century AD. In 1066, the last crowned <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxon</a> monarch, <a href="/wiki/Harold_Godwinson" title="Harold Godwinson">Harold II</a>, was <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hastings" title="Battle of Hastings">defeated and killed</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">Norman conquest of England</a> and the English monarchy passed to the Normans' victorious leader, <a href="/wiki/William_the_Conqueror" title="William the Conqueror">William the Conqueror</a>, and his descendants.</p>
<p>In the 13th century, <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, as <a href="/wiki/Prince_of_Wales" title="Prince of Wales">a principality</a>, became a <a href="/wiki/Client_state" title="Client state">client state</a> of the English kingdom, while <i><a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta" title="Magna Carta">Magna Carta</a></i> began a process of reducing the English monarch's political powers.</p>
<p>From 1603, when the Scottish monarch <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_James_VI" title="King James VI">King James VI</a> inherited the English throne as James I, both the English and Scottish kingdoms were ruled by a single sovereign. From 1649 to 1660, the tradition of monarchy was broken by the republican <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_England" title="Commonwealth of England">Commonwealth of England</a>, which followed the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Wars of the Three Kingdoms">Wars of the Three Kingdoms</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Settlement_1701" title="Act of Settlement 1701">Act of Settlement 1701</a>, which is still in force, excluded <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholics" title="Roman Catholics">Roman Catholics</a>, or those who marry Catholics, from succession to the English throne. In 1707, the kingdoms of England and Scotland were merged to create the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Kingdom of Great Britain</a>, and in 1801, the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Kingdom of Ireland</a> joined to create the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland</a>. The British monarch became nominal head of the vast <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, which covered a quarter of the world's surface at its greatest extent in 1921.</p>
<p>In the 1920s, five-sixths of Ireland seceded from the Union as the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Free_State" title="Irish Free State">Irish Free State</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Balfour_Declaration_of_1926" title="Balfour Declaration of 1926">Balfour Declaration</a> recognised the evolution of the <a href="/wiki/Dominion" title="Dominion">dominions</a> of the empire into separate, self-governing countries within a <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a>. After the <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World War</a>, the vast majority of British colonies and territories became independent, effectively bringing the empire to an end. George VI and his successor, Elizabeth II, adopted the title <a href="/wiki/Head_of_the_Commonwealth" title="Head of the Commonwealth">Head of the Commonwealth</a> as a symbol of the free association of its independent member states.</p>
<p>The United Kingdom and fifteen other Commonwealth monarchies that share the same person as their monarch are called <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_realm" title="Commonwealth realm">Commonwealth realms</a>. The terms <i>British monarchy</i> and <i>British monarch</i> are frequently still employed in reference to the shared individual and institution; however, each country is sovereign and independent of the others, and the monarch has a different, specific, and official national <a href="/wiki/List_of_titles_and_honours_of_Queen_Elizabeth_II#Royal_titles_and_styles" title="List of titles and honours of Queen Elizabeth II">title and style</a> for each realm.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Constitutional_role">Constitutional role</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Constitutional role">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the uncodified <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Constitution of the United Kingdom">Constitution of the United Kingdom</a>, the Monarch (otherwise referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">Sovereign</a> or "His/Her <a href="/wiki/Majesty" title="Majesty">Majesty</a>", abbreviated H.M.) is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Head_of_State" title="Head of State">Head of State</a>. <a href="/wiki/Oath_of_allegiance" title="Oath of allegiance">Oaths of allegiance</a> are made to the Queen and her lawful successors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> "<a href="/wiki/God_Save_the_Queen" title="God Save the Queen">God Save the Queen</a>" (or "God Save the King") is the British <a href="/wiki/National_anthem" title="National anthem">national anthem</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> and the monarch appears on postage stamps, coins and banknotes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Monarch takes little direct part in Government. The decisions to exercise <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sovereign" title="Sovereign">sovereign</a> powers are delegated from the Monarch, either by <a href="/wiki/Statute" title="Statute">statute</a> or by <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_convention_(political_custom)" title="Constitutional convention (political custom)">convention</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_the_Crown" title="Minister of the Crown">Ministers</a> or officers of <a href="/wiki/The_Crown" title="The Crown">the Crown</a>, or other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_body" title="Public body">public bodies</a>, exclusive of the Monarch personally. Thus the acts of state done in the name of the Crown, such as Crown Appointments,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> even if personally performed by the Monarch, such as the <a href="/wiki/Speech_from_the_throne" title="Speech from the throne">Queen's Speech</a> and the <a href="/wiki/State_Opening_of_Parliament" title="State Opening of Parliament">State Opening of Parliament</a>, depend upon decisions made elsewhere:</p>
<ul>
<li>Legislative power is exercised by the <a href="/wiki/Queen-in-Parliament" title="Queen-in-Parliament">Queen-in-Parliament</a>, by and with the advice and consent of <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Parliament</a>, the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> and the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a>.</li>
<li>Executive power is exercised by <a href="/wiki/Government_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Government of the United Kingdom">Her Majesty's Government</a>, which comprises <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_the_Crown" title="Minister of the Crown">Ministers</a>, primarily the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Cabinet of the United Kingdom">Cabinet</a>, which is technically a committee of the <a href="/wiki/Privy_Council_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Privy Council of the United Kingdom">Privy Council</a>. They have the direction of the <a href="/wiki/British_Armed_Forces" title="British Armed Forces">Armed Forces of the Crown</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Civil_Service" title="Her Majesty's Civil Service">the Civil Service</a> and other Crown Servants such as the <a href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Diplomatic_Service" title="Her Majesty's Diplomatic Service">Diplomatic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Secret_Intelligence_Service" title="Secret Intelligence Service">Secret Services</a> (the Queen receives certain foreign intelligence reports before the Prime Minister does<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>).</li>
<li>Judicial power is vested in the <a href="/wiki/Judiciary_of_England_and_Wales" title="Judiciary of England and Wales">Judiciary</a>, who by constitution and statute<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> have <a href="/wiki/Judicial_independence" title="Judicial independence">judicial independence</a> of the Government.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>, of which the Monarch is the head, has its own legislative, judicial and executive structures.</li>
<li>Powers independent of government are legally granted to other public bodies by statute or <a href="/wiki/Statutory_Instrument" title="Statutory Instrument">Statutory Instrument</a> such as an <a href="/wiki/Order_in_Council" title="Order in Council">Order in Council</a>, <a href="/wiki/Royal_Commission" title="Royal Commission">Royal Commission</a> or otherwise.</li>
</ul>
<p>The Sovereign's role as a constitutional monarch is largely limited to non-partisan functions, such as granting <a href="/wiki/Orders,_decorations,_and_medals_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Orders, decorations, and medals of the United Kingdom">honours</a>. This role has been recognised since the 19th century. The constitutional writer <a href="/wiki/Walter_Bagehot" title="Walter Bagehot">Walter Bagehot</a> identified the monarchy in 1867 as the "dignified part" rather than the "efficient part" of government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:English_Bill_of_Rights_of_1689.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="614" data-file-width="304" height="444" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/English_Bill_of_Rights_of_1689.jpg/220px-English_Bill_of_Rights_of_1689.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e3/English_Bill_of_Rights_of_1689.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:English_Bill_of_Rights_of_1689.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The English <a href="/wiki/Bill_of_Rights_1689" title="Bill of Rights 1689">Bill of Rights of 1689</a> curtailed the monarch's governmental power.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Appointment_of_the_Prime_Minister">Appointment of the Prime Minister</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Appointment of the Prime Minister">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Whenever necessary, the Monarch is responsible for appointing a new Prime Minister (who by convention appoints and may dismiss every other <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_the_Crown" title="Minister of the Crown">Minister of the Crown</a>, and thereby constitutes and controls the government). In accordance with unwritten constitutional conventions, the Sovereign must appoint an individual who commands the support of the House of Commons, usually the leader of the party or coalition that has a majority in that House. The Prime Minister takes office by attending the Monarch in private audience, and after "<a href="/wiki/Kissing_hands" title="Kissing hands">kissing hands</a>" that appointment is immediately effective without any other formality or instrument.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a <a href="/wiki/Hung_parliament" title="Hung parliament">hung parliament</a> where no party or coalition holds a majority, the monarch has an increased degree of latitude in choosing the individual likely to command the most support, though it would usually be the leader of the largest party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Since 1945, there have only been two hung parliaments. The first followed the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_February_1974" title="United Kingdom general election, February 1974">February 1974 general election</a> when <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a> was appointed Prime Minister after <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a> resigned following his failure to form a coalition. Although Wilson's <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> did not have a majority, they were the largest party. The second followed the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2010" title="United Kingdom general election, 2010">May 2010 general election</a>, in which the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservatives</a> (the largest party) and <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats" title="Liberal Democrats">Liberal Democrats</a> (the third largest party) agreed to form the first coalition government since World War II.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dissolution_of_Parliament">Dissolution of Parliament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Dissolution of Parliament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1950 the King's <a href="/wiki/Private_Secretary" title="Private Secretary">Private Secretary</a> writing pseudonymously to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> newspaper asserted a constitutional convention: according to the <a href="/wiki/Lascelles_Principles" title="Lascelles Principles">Lascelles Principles</a>, if a minority government asked to dissolve Parliament to call an early election to strengthen its position, the monarch could refuse, and would do so under three conditions. When Prime Minister Wilson requested a dissolution late in 1974, the Queen granted his request as Heath had already failed to form a coalition. The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_October_1974" title="United Kingdom general election, October 1974">resulting general election</a> gave Wilson a small majority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The monarch could in theory unilaterally dismiss a Prime Minister, but a Prime Minister's term now comes to an end only by electoral defeat, death, or resignation. The last monarch to remove a Prime Minister was <a href="/wiki/William_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="William IV of the United Kingdom">William IV</a>, who dismissed <a href="/wiki/William_Lamb,_2nd_Viscount_Melbourne" title="William Lamb, 2nd Viscount Melbourne">Lord Melbourne</a> in 1834.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Fixed-term_Parliaments_Act_2011" title="Fixed-term Parliaments Act 2011">Fixed-term Parliaments Act 2011</a> removed the monarch's authority to dissolve Parliament; the Act specifically retained the monarch's power of <i>prorogation</i> however, which is a regular <a href="/wiki/Legislative_session#United_Kingdom" title="Legislative session">feature of the parliamentary calendar</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Royal_Prerogative">Royal Prerogative</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Royal Prerogative">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Royal_prerogative_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Royal prerogative in the United Kingdom">Royal prerogative in the United Kingdom</a></div>
<p>Some of the government's executive authority is theoretically and nominally vested in the Sovereign and is known as the <a href="/wiki/Royal_prerogative" title="Royal prerogative">royal prerogative</a>. The monarch acts within the constraints of convention and precedent, exercising prerogative only on the advice of ministers responsible to Parliament, often through the Prime Minister or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Most_Honourable_Privy_Council" title="Her Majesty's Most Honourable Privy Council">Privy Council</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parl_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-parl-13">[13]</a></sup> In practice, prerogative powers are exercised only on the Prime Minister's advice – the Prime Minister, and not the Sovereign, has control. The monarch holds a weekly audience with the Prime Minister. No records of these audiences are taken and the proceedings remain fully confidential.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The monarch may express his or her views, but, as a constitutional ruler, must ultimately accept the decisions of the Prime Minister and the Cabinet (providing they command the support of the House). In Bagehot's words: "the Sovereign has, under a constitutional monarchy ... three rights – the right to be consulted, the right to encourage, the right to warn."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although the Royal Prerogative is extensive and parliamentary approval is not formally required for its exercise, it is limited. Many Crown prerogatives have fallen out of use or have been permanently transferred to Parliament. For example, the monarch cannot impose and collect new taxes; such an action requires the authorisation of an Act of Parliament. According to a parliamentary report, "The Crown cannot invent new prerogative powers", and Parliament can override any prerogative power by passing legislation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PASC_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-PASC-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Royal Prerogative includes the powers to appoint and dismiss ministers, regulate the civil service, issue passports, declare war, make peace, direct the actions of the military, and negotiate and ratify treaties, alliances, and international agreements. However, a treaty cannot alter the domestic laws of the United Kingdom; an Act of Parliament is necessary in such cases. The monarch is <a href="/wiki/Commander-in-chief" title="Commander-in-chief">commander-in-chief</a> of the Armed Forces (the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a>), accredits British <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_Commissioner" title="High Commissioner">High Commissioners</a> and ambassadors, and receives diplomats from foreign states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PASC_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-PASC-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>It is the prerogative of the monarch to summon and <a href="/wiki/Legislative_session#Procedure_in_Commonwealth_realms" title="Legislative session">prorogue</a> Parliament. Each parliamentary session begins with the monarch's summons. The new parliamentary session is marked by the <a href="/wiki/State_Opening_of_Parliament" title="State Opening of Parliament">State Opening of Parliament</a>, during which the Sovereign reads the <a href="/wiki/Speech_from_the_throne" title="Speech from the throne">Speech from the throne</a> in the Chamber of the House of Lords, outlining the Government's legislative agenda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Prorogation usually occurs about one year after a session begins, and formally concludes the session.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Dissolution ends a parliamentary term, and is followed by a general election for all seats in the House of Commons. A general election is normally held five years after the previous one under the <a href="/wiki/Fixed-term_Parliaments_Act_2011" title="Fixed-term Parliaments Act 2011">Fixed-term Parliaments Act 2011</a>, but can be held sooner if the Prime Minister loses a motion of confidence, or if two-thirds of the members of the House of Commons vote to hold an early election.</p>
<p>Before a bill passed by the legislative Houses can become law, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_assent" title="Royal assent">royal assent</a> (the monarch's approval) is required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> In theory, assent can either be granted (making the bill law) or withheld (vetoing the bill), but since 1707 assent has always been granted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>The monarch has a similar relationship with the <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolved</a> governments of Scotland, Wales, and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>. The Sovereign appoints the <a href="/wiki/First_Minister_of_Scotland" title="First Minister of Scotland">First Minister of Scotland</a> on the nomination of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament" title="Scottish Parliament">Scottish Parliament</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/First_Minister_of_Wales" title="First Minister of Wales">First Minister of Wales</a> on the nomination of the <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_for_Wales" title="National Assembly for Wales">National Assembly for Wales</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> In Scottish matters, the Sovereign acts on the advice of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Government" title="Scottish Government">Scottish Government</a>. However, as devolution is more limited in Wales, in Welsh matters the Sovereign acts on the advice of the Prime Minister and Cabinet of the United Kingdom. The Sovereign can veto any law passed by the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Assembly" title="Northern Ireland Assembly">Northern Ireland Assembly</a>, if it is deemed unconstitutional by the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Northern_Ireland" title="Secretary of State for Northern Ireland">Secretary of State for Northern Ireland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Sovereign is deemed the "fount of justice"; although the Sovereign does not personally rule in judicial cases, judicial functions are performed in his or her name. For instance, prosecutions are brought on the monarch's behalf, and courts derive their authority from the Crown. The common law holds that the Sovereign "can do no wrong"; the monarch cannot be prosecuted for criminal offences. The <a href="/wiki/Crown_Proceedings_Act_1947" title="Crown Proceedings Act 1947">Crown Proceedings Act 1947</a> allows civil lawsuits against the Crown in its public capacity (that is, lawsuits against the government), but not lawsuits against the monarch personally. The Sovereign exercises the "prerogative of mercy", which is used to <a href="/wiki/Pardon" title="Pardon">pardon</a> convicted offenders or reduce sentences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parl_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-parl-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PASC_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-PASC-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>The monarch is the "<a href="/wiki/Fount_of_honour" title="Fount of honour">fount of honour</a>", the source of all honours and dignities in the United Kingdom. The Crown creates all <a href="/wiki/Peerage" title="Peerage">peerages</a>, appoints members of the <a href="/wiki/Knight" title="Knight">orders of chivalry</a>, grants knighthoods and awards other honours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Although peerages and most other honours are granted on the advice of the Prime Minister, some honours are within the personal gift of the Sovereign, and are not granted on ministerial advice. The monarch alone appoints members of the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Garter" title="Order of the Garter">Order of the Garter</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Thistle" title="Order of the Thistle">Order of the Thistle</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Victorian_Order" title="Royal Victorian Order">Royal Victorian Order</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Order_of_Merit_(Commonwealth)" title="Order of Merit (Commonwealth)">Order of Merit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="English_monarchy">English monarchy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: English monarchy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_monarchs" title="List of English monarchs">List of English monarchs</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bayeux_Tapestry_WillelmDux.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="291" data-file-width="401" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Bayeux_Tapestry_WillelmDux.jpg/220px-Bayeux_Tapestry_WillelmDux.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Bayeux_Tapestry_WillelmDux.jpg/330px-Bayeux_Tapestry_WillelmDux.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/96/Bayeux_Tapestry_WillelmDux.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bayeux_Tapestry_WillelmDux.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Bayeux_Tapestry" title="Bayeux Tapestry">Bayeux Tapestry</a> depicts the Norman Conquest of 1066.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following Viking raids and settlement in the ninth century, the Anglo-Saxon kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Wessex" title="Wessex">Wessex</a> emerged as the dominant English kingdom. <a href="/wiki/Alfred_the_Great" title="Alfred the Great">Alfred the Great</a> secured Wessex, achieved dominance over western <a href="/wiki/Mercia" title="Mercia">Mercia</a>, and assumed the title "King of the English".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> His grandson <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelstan" title="Æthelstan">Æthelstan</a> was the first king to rule over a unitary kingdom roughly corresponding to the present borders of England, though its constituent parts retained strong regional identities. The 11th century saw England become more stable, despite a number of wars with the Danes, which resulted in a Danish monarchy for one generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The conquest of England in 1066 by <a href="/wiki/William_the_Conqueror" title="William the Conqueror">William, Duke of Normandy</a>, was crucial in terms of both political and social change. The new monarch continued the centralisation of power begun in the Anglo-Saxon period, while the <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">Feudal System</a> continued to develop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>William was succeeded by two of his sons: <a href="/wiki/William_II_of_England" title="William II of England">William II</a>, then <a href="/wiki/Henry_I_of_England" title="Henry I of England">Henry I</a>. Henry made a controversial decision to name his daughter <a href="/wiki/Empress_Matilda" title="Empress Matilda">Matilda</a> (his only surviving child) as his heir. Following Henry's death in 1135, one of William I's grandsons, <a href="/wiki/Stephen,_King_of_England" title="Stephen, King of England">Stephen</a>, laid claim to the throne and took power with the support of most of the <a href="/wiki/Baron#Barons_in_the_United_Kingdom_and_the_Commonwealth" title="Baron">barons</a>. Matilda challenged his reign; as a result, England descended into a period of disorder known as <a href="/wiki/The_Anarchy" title="The Anarchy">the Anarchy</a>. Stephen maintained a precarious hold on power, but agreed to a compromise under which Matilda's son <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">Henry</a> would succeed him. Henry accordingly became the first <a href="/wiki/Angevin_kings_of_England" title="Angevin kings of England">Angevin king of England</a> and the first monarch of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plantagenet_dynasty" title="Plantagenet dynasty">Plantagenet dynasty</a> as Henry II in 1154.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>The reigns of most of the Angevin monarchs were marred by civil strife and conflicts between the monarch and the nobility. Henry II faced rebellions from his own sons, the future monarchs <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Richard_I" title="Richard I">Richard I</a> and <a href="/wiki/John,_King_of_England" title="John, King of England">John</a>. Nevertheless, Henry managed to expand his kingdom, forming what is retrospectively known as the <a href="/wiki/Angevin_Empire" title="Angevin Empire">Angevin Empire</a>. Upon Henry's death, his elder son Richard succeeded to the throne; he was absent from England for most of his reign, as he left to fight in the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a>. He was killed besieging a castle, and John succeeded him.</p>
<p>John's reign was marked by conflict with the barons, particularly over the limits of royal power. In 1215, the barons coerced the king into issuing <a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta" title="Magna Carta">Magna Carta</a> (<a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> for "Great Charter") to guarantee the rights and liberties of the nobility. Soon afterwards, further disagreements plunged England into a civil war known as the <a href="/wiki/First_Barons%27_War" title="First Barons' War">First Barons' War</a>. The war came to an abrupt end after John died in 1216, leaving the Crown to his nine-year-old son <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_England" title="Henry III of England">Henry III</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Later in Henry's reign, <a href="/wiki/Simon_de_Montfort,_6th_Earl_of_Leicester" title="Simon de Montfort, 6th Earl of Leicester">Simon de Montfort</a> led the barons in another rebellion, beginning the <a href="/wiki/Second_Barons%27_War" title="Second Barons' War">Second Barons' War</a>. The war ended in a clear royalist victory and in the death of many rebels, but not before the king had agreed to summon a parliament in 1265.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>The next monarch, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_Longshanks" title="Edward Longshanks">Edward Longshanks</a>, was far more successful in maintaining royal power and responsible for the <a href="/wiki/Conquest_of_Wales_by_Edward_I" title="Conquest of Wales by Edward I">conquest of Wales</a>. He attempted to establish English domination of Scotland. However, gains in Scotland were reversed during the reign of his successor, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_II" title="Edward II">Edward II</a>, who also faced conflict with the nobility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> In 1311, Edward II was forced to relinquish many of his powers to a committee of <a href="/wiki/Ordinances_of_1311" title="Ordinances of 1311">baronial "ordainers"</a>; however, military victories helped him regain control in 1322.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Nevertheless, in 1327, Edward was deposed by his wife <a href="/wiki/Isabella_of_France" title="Isabella of France">Isabella</a>. His 14-year-old son became <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_III" title="Edward III">Edward III</a>. Edward III claimed the French Crown, setting off the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War" title="Hundred Years' War">Hundred Years' War</a> between England and France.</p>
<p>His campaigns conquered much French territory, but by 1374, all the gains had been lost. Edward's reign was also marked by the further development of Parliament, which came to be divided into two Houses. In 1377, Edward III died, leaving the Crown to his 10-year-old grandson <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Richard_II" title="Richard II">Richard II</a>. Like many of his predecessors, Richard II conflicted with the nobles by attempting to concentrate power in his own hands. In 1399, while he was campaigning in Ireland, his cousin <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_Bolingbroke" title="Henry Bolingbroke">Henry Bolingbroke</a> seized power. Richard was deposed, imprisoned, and eventually murdered, probably by starvation, and Henry became king as Henry IV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Henry IV was the grandson of Edward III and the son of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_of_Gaunt,_Duke_of_Lancaster" title="John of Gaunt, Duke of Lancaster">John of Gaunt, Duke of Lancaster</a>; hence, his dynasty was known as the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lancaster" title="House of Lancaster">House of Lancaster</a>. For most of his reign, Henry IV was forced to fight off plots and rebellions; his success was partly due to the military skill of his son, the future <a href="/wiki/Henry_V_of_England" title="Henry V of England">Henry V</a>. Henry V's own reign, which began in 1413, was largely free from domestic strife, leaving the king free to pursue the Hundred Years' War in France. Although he was victorious, his sudden death in 1422 left his infant son <a href="/wiki/Henry_VI_of_England" title="Henry VI of England">Henry VI</a> on the throne and gave the French an opportunity to overthrow English rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>The unpopularity of Henry VI's counsellors and his belligerent consort, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_of_Anjou" title="Margaret of Anjou">Margaret of Anjou</a>, as well as his own ineffectual leadership, led to the weakening of the House of Lancaster. The Lancastrians faced a challenge from the House of York, so called because its head, a descendant of Edward III, was <a href="/wiki/Richard_of_York,_3rd_Duke_of_York" title="Richard of York, 3rd Duke of York">Richard, Duke of York</a>. Although the Duke of York died in battle in 1460, his eldest son, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_IV" title="Edward IV">Edward IV</a>, led the Yorkists to victory in 1461. The <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Roses" title="Wars of the Roses">Wars of the Roses</a>, nevertheless, continued intermittently during his reign and those of his son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_V" title="Edward V">Edward V</a> and brother <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Richard_III" title="Richard III">Richard III</a>. Edward V disappeared, presumably murdered by Richard. Ultimately, the conflict culminated in success for the Lancastrian branch led by <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">Henry Tudor</a>, in 1485, when Richard III was killed in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bosworth_Field" title="Battle of Bosworth Field">Battle of Bosworth Field</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Now King Henry VII, he neutralised the remaining Yorkist forces, partly by marrying <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_of_York" title="Elizabeth of York">Elizabeth of York</a>, a Yorkist heir. Through skill and ability, Henry re-established absolute supremacy in the realm, and the conflicts with the nobility that had plagued previous monarchs came to an end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The reign of the second Tudor king, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_VIII" title="Henry VIII">Henry VIII</a>, was one of great political change. Religious upheaval and disputes with the <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">Pope</a> led the monarch to break from the Roman Catholic Church and to establish the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> (the Anglican Church).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wales – which had been conquered centuries earlier, but had remained a separate dominion – was annexed to England under the <a href="/wiki/Laws_in_Wales_Acts_1535_and_1542" title="Laws in Wales Acts 1535 and 1542">Laws in Wales Acts 1535 and 1542</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Henry VIII's son and successor, the young <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_VI" title="Edward VI">Edward VI</a>, continued with further religious reforms, but his early death in 1553 precipitated a succession crisis. He was wary of allowing his Catholic elder half-sister <a href="/wiki/Mary_I_of_England" title="Mary I of England">Mary I</a> to succeed, and therefore drew up a will designating <a href="/wiki/Lady_Jane_Grey" title="Lady Jane Grey">Lady Jane Grey</a> as his heiress. Jane's reign, however, lasted only nine days; with tremendous popular support, Mary deposed her and declared herself the lawful sovereign. Mary I married <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Spain" title="Philip II of Spain">Philip of Spain</a>, who was declared king and co-ruler, pursued disastrous wars in France, and attempted to return England to Roman Catholicism, burning Protestants at the stake as heretics in the process. Upon her death in 1558, the pair were succeeded by her Protestant half-sister <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I" title="Elizabeth I">Elizabeth I</a>. England returned to Protestantism and continued its growth into a major world power by building its navy and exploring the New World.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scottish_monarchy">Scottish monarchy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Scottish monarchy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Scottish_monarchs" title="List of Scottish monarchs">List of Scottish monarchs</a></div>
<p>In Scotland, as in England, monarchies emerged after the withdrawal of the Roman empire from Britain in the early fifth century. The three groups that lived in Scotland at this time were the <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">Picts</a> in the north east, the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Britons" title="Celtic Britons">Britons</a> in the south, including the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Strathclyde" title="Kingdom of Strathclyde">Kingdom of Strathclyde</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaels</a> or <a href="/wiki/Scoti" title="Scoti">Scotti</a> (who would later give their name to Scotland), of the Irish petty kingdom of <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A1l_Riata" title="Dál Riata">Dál Riata</a> in the west. <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_MacAlpin" title="Kenneth MacAlpin">Kenneth MacAlpin</a> is traditionally viewed as the first king of a united Scotland (known as Scotia to writers in Latin, or <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Alba" title="Kingdom of Alba">Alba</a> to the Scots).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The expansion of Scottish dominions continued over the next two centuries, as other territories such as Strathclyde were absorbed.</p>
<p>Early Scottish monarchs did not inherit the Crown directly; instead the custom of <a href="/wiki/Tanistry" title="Tanistry">tanistry</a> was followed, where the monarchy alternated between different branches of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Alpin" title="House of Alpin">House of Alpin</a>. As a result, however, the rival dynastic lines clashed, often violently. From 942 to 1005, seven consecutive monarchs were either murdered or killed in battle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> In 1005, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malcolm_II" title="Malcolm II">Malcolm II</a> ascended the throne having killed many rivals. He continued to ruthlessly eliminate opposition, and when he died in 1034 he was succeeded by his grandson, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Duncan_I" title="Duncan I">Duncan I</a>, instead of a cousin, as had been usual. In 1040, Duncan suffered defeat in battle at the hands of <a href="/wiki/Macbeth,_King_of_Scotland" title="Macbeth, King of Scotland">Macbeth</a>, who was killed himself in 1057 by Duncan's son <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_III_of_Scotland" title="Malcolm III of Scotland">Malcolm</a>. The following year, after killing Macbeth's stepson <a href="/wiki/Lulach" title="Lulach">Lulach</a>, Malcolm ascended the throne as Malcolm III.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>With a further series of battles and deposings, five of Malcolm's sons as well as one of his brothers successively became king. Eventually, the Crown came to his youngest son, <a href="/wiki/David_I_of_Scotland" title="David I of Scotland">David I</a>. David was succeeded by his grandsons <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malcolm_IV" title="Malcolm IV">Malcolm IV</a>, and then by <a href="/wiki/William_the_Lion" title="William the Lion">William the Lion</a>, the longest-reigning King of Scots before the <a href="/wiki/Union_of_the_Crowns" title="Union of the Crowns">Union of the Crowns</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> William participated in a rebellion against King Henry II of England but when the rebellion failed, William was captured by the English. In exchange for his release, William was forced to acknowledge Henry as his feudal overlord. The English King Richard I agreed to terminate the arrangement in 1189, in return for a large sum of money needed for the Crusades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> William died in 1214, and was succeeded by his son <a href="/wiki/Alexander_II_of_Scotland" title="Alexander II of Scotland">Alexander II</a>. Alexander II, as well as his successor <a href="/wiki/Alexander_III_of_Scotland" title="Alexander III of Scotland">Alexander III</a>, attempted to take over the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Isles" title="Western Isles">Western Isles</a>, which were still under the overlordship of Norway. During the reign of Alexander III, Norway launched an unsuccessful invasion of Scotland; the ensuing <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Perth" title="Treaty of Perth">Treaty of Perth</a> recognised Scottish control of the Western Isles and other disputed areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Alexander III's unexpected death in a riding accident in 1286 precipitated a major succession crisis. Scottish leaders appealed to King Edward I of England for help in determining who was the rightful heir. Edward chose Alexander's three-year-old Norwegian granddaughter, <a href="/wiki/Margaret,_Maid_of_Norway" title="Margaret, Maid of Norway">Margaret</a>. On her way to Scotland in 1290, however, Margaret died at sea, and Edward was again asked to adjudicate between <a href="/wiki/Competitors_for_the_Crown_of_Scotland" title="Competitors for the Crown of Scotland">13 rival claimants to the throne</a>. A court was set up and after two years of deliberation, it pronounced <a href="/wiki/John_Balliol" title="John Balliol">John Balliol</a> to be king. However, Edward proceeded to treat Balliol as a vassal, and tried to exert influence over Scotland. In 1295, when Balliol renounced his allegiance to England, Edward I invaded. During the first ten years of the ensuing <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_Scottish_Independence" title="Wars of Scottish Independence">Wars of Scottish Independence</a>, Scotland had no monarch, until <a href="/wiki/Robert_the_Bruce" title="Robert the Bruce">Robert the Bruce</a> declared himself king in 1306.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Robert's efforts to control Scotland culminated in success, and Scottish independence was acknowledged in 1328. However, only one year later, Robert died and was succeeded by his five-year-old son, <a href="/wiki/David_II_of_Scotland" title="David II of Scotland">David II</a>. On the pretext of restoring John Balliol's rightful heir, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Balliol" title="Edward Balliol">Edward Balliol</a>, the English again invaded in 1332. During the next four years, Balliol was crowned, deposed, restored, deposed, restored, and deposed until he eventually settled in England, and David remained king for the next 35 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>David II died childless in 1371 and was succeeded by his nephew <a href="/wiki/Robert_II_of_Scotland" title="Robert II of Scotland">Robert II</a> of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Stuart" title="House of Stuart">House of Stuart</a>. The reigns of both Robert II and his successor, <a href="/wiki/Robert_III_of_Scotland" title="Robert III of Scotland">Robert III</a>, were marked by a general decline in royal power. When Robert III died in 1406, <a href="/wiki/Regent" title="Regent">regents</a> had to rule the country; the monarch, Robert III's son <a href="/wiki/James_I_of_Scotland" title="James I of Scotland">James I</a>, had been taken captive by the English. Having paid a large ransom, James returned to Scotland in 1424; to restore his authority, he used ruthless measures, including the execution of several of his enemies. He was assassinated by a group of nobles. <a href="/wiki/James_II_of_Scotland" title="James II of Scotland">James II</a> continued his father's policies by subduing influential noblemen but he was killed in an accident at the age of thirty, and a council of regents again assumed power. <a href="/wiki/James_III_of_Scotland" title="James III of Scotland">James III</a> was defeated in a battle against rebellious Scottish earls in 1488, leading to another boy-king: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_IV" title="James IV">James IV</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1513 James IV launched an invasion of England, attempting to take advantage of the absence of the English King Henry VIII. His forces met with disaster at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Flodden" title="Battle of Flodden">Flodden Field</a>; the King, many senior noblemen, and hundreds of soldiers were killed. As his son and successor, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_V" title="James V">James V</a>, was an infant, the government was again taken over by regents. James V led another disastrous war with the English in 1542, and his death in the same year left the Crown in the hands of his six-day-old daughter, <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Queen_of_Scots" title="Mary, Queen of Scots">Mary I</a>. Once again, a regency was established.</p>
<p>Mary, a Roman Catholic, reigned during a period of great religious upheaval in Scotland. As a result of the efforts of reformers such as <a href="/wiki/John_Knox" title="John Knox">John Knox</a>, a Protestant ascendancy was established. Mary caused alarm by marrying her Catholic cousin, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Stuart,_Lord_Darnley" title="Henry Stuart, Lord Darnley">Lord Darnley</a>, in 1565. After Lord Darnley's assassination in 1567, Mary contracted an even more unpopular marriage with the <a href="/wiki/James_Hepburn,_4th_Earl_of_Bothwell" title="James Hepburn, 4th Earl of Bothwell">Earl of Bothwell</a>, who was widely suspected of Darnley's murder. The nobility rebelled against the Queen, forcing her to abdicate. She fled to England, and the Crown went to her infant son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_VI" title="James VI">James VI</a>, who was brought up as a Protestant. Mary was imprisoned and later executed by the English queen Elizabeth I.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_union_and_republican_phase">Personal union and republican phase</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Personal union and republican phase">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JamesIEngland.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2952" data-file-width="1750" height="371" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/JamesIEngland.jpg/220px-JamesIEngland.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/JamesIEngland.jpg/330px-JamesIEngland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/JamesIEngland.jpg/440px-JamesIEngland.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:JamesIEngland.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
In 1603 James VI and I became the first monarch to rule over England, Scotland, and Ireland together.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Elizabeth I's death in 1603 ended Tudor rule in England. Since she had no children, she was succeeded by the Scottish monarch <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_VI" title="James VI">James VI</a>, who was the great-grandson of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_VIII" title="Henry VIII">Henry VIII</a>'s older sister and hence Elizabeth's first cousin twice removed. James VI ruled in England as James I after what was known as the "<a href="/wiki/Union_of_the_Crowns" title="Union of the Crowns">Union of the Crowns</a>". Although England and Scotland were in <a href="/wiki/Personal_union" title="Personal union">personal union</a> under one monarch – James I became the first monarch to style himself "King of Great Britain" in 1604<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> – they remained two separate kingdoms. James I's successor, <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">Charles I</a>, experienced frequent conflicts with the English Parliament related to the issue of royal and parliamentary powers, especially the power to impose taxes. He provoked opposition by ruling without Parliament from 1629 to 1640, unilaterally levying taxes and adopting controversial religious policies (many of which were offensive to the Scottish <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Presbyterians" title="Presbyterians">Presbyterians</a> and the English <a href="/wiki/Puritans" title="Puritans">Puritans</a>). His attempt to enforce <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a> led to organised rebellion in Scotland (the "<a href="/wiki/Bishops%27_Wars" title="Bishops' Wars">Bishops' Wars</a>") and ignited the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Wars of the Three Kingdoms">Wars of the Three Kingdoms</a>. In 1642, the conflict between the King and English Parliament reached its climax and the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a> began.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Civil War culminated in the execution of the king in 1649, the overthrow of the English monarchy, and the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_England" title="Commonwealth of England">Commonwealth of England</a>. Charles I's son, <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">Charles II</a>, was proclaimed King of Great Britain in Scotland, but he was forced to flee abroad after he invaded England and was defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Worcester" title="Battle of Worcester">Battle of Worcester</a>. In 1653, <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a>, the most prominent military and political leader in the nation, seized power and declared himself <a href="/wiki/Lord_Protector" title="Lord Protector">Lord Protector</a> (effectively becoming a military dictator, but refusing the title of king). Cromwell ruled until his death in 1658, when he was succeeded by his son <a href="/wiki/Richard_Cromwell" title="Richard Cromwell">Richard</a>. The new Lord Protector had little interest in governing; he soon resigned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The lack of clear leadership led to civil and military unrest, and for a popular desire to restore the monarchy. In 1660, the <a href="/wiki/Restoration_(England)" title="Restoration (England)">monarchy was restored</a> and Charles II returned to Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Charles II's reign was marked by the development of the first modern political parties in England. Charles had no legitimate children, and was due to be succeeded by his Roman Catholic brother, <a href="/wiki/James_II_of_England" title="James II of England">James, Duke of York</a>. A parliamentary effort to exclude James from the <a href="/wiki/Succession_to_the_British_throne" title="Succession to the British throne">line of succession</a> arose; the "Petitioners", who supported exclusion, became the Whig Party, whereas the "Abhorrers", who opposed exclusion, became the Tory Party. The Exclusion Bill failed; on several occasions, Charles II dissolved Parliament because he feared that the bill might pass. After the dissolution of the Parliament of 1681, Charles ruled without a Parliament until his death in 1685. When James succeeded Charles, he pursued a policy of offering religious tolerance to Roman Catholics, thereby drawing the ire of many of his Protestant subjects. Many opposed James's decisions to maintain a large standing army, to appoint Roman Catholics to high political and military offices, and to imprison <a href="/wiki/Seven_Bishops" title="Seven Bishops">Church of England clerics who challenged his policies</a>. As a result, a group of Protestants known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immortal_Seven" title="Immortal Seven">Immortal Seven</a> invited James II's daughter <a href="/wiki/Mary_II_of_England" title="Mary II of England">Mary</a> and her husband <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_III_of_Orange" title="William III of Orange">William III of Orange</a> to depose the king. William obliged, arriving in England on 5 November 1688 to great public support. Faced with the defection of many of his Protestant officials, James fled the realm and William and Mary (rather than <a href="/wiki/James_Francis_Edward_Stuart" title="James Francis Edward Stuart">James II's Catholic son</a>) were declared joint Sovereigns of England, Scotland and Ireland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>James's overthrow, known as the <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a>, was one of the most important events in the long evolution of parliamentary power. The <a href="/wiki/Bill_of_Rights_1689" title="Bill of Rights 1689">Bill of Rights 1689</a> affirmed parliamentary supremacy, and declared that the English people held certain rights, including the freedom from taxes imposed without parliamentary consent. The Bill of Rights required future monarchs to be Protestants, and provided that, after any children of William and Mary, Mary's sister <a href="/wiki/Anne,_Queen_of_Great_Britain" title="Anne, Queen of Great Britain">Anne</a> would inherit the Crown. Mary died childless in 1694, leaving William as the sole monarch. By 1700, a political crisis arose, as all of Anne's children had died, leaving her as the only individual left in the line of succession. Parliament was afraid that the former James II or his supporters, known as <a href="/wiki/Jacobitism" title="Jacobitism">Jacobites</a>, might attempt to reclaim the throne. Parliament passed the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Settlement_1701" title="Act of Settlement 1701">Act of Settlement 1701</a>, which excluded James and his Catholic relations from the succession and made William's nearest Protestant relations, the family of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sophia,_Electress_of_Hanover" title="Sophia, Electress of Hanover">Sophia, Electress of Hanover</a>, next in line to the throne after his sister-in-law Anne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Soon after the passage of the Act, William III died, leaving the Crown to Anne.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="After_the_1707_Acts_of_Union">After the 1707 Acts of Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: After the 1707 Acts of Union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anniex.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1287" height="274" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Anniex.jpg/220px-Anniex.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Anniex.jpg/330px-Anniex.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Anniex.jpg/440px-Anniex.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anniex.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
England and Scotland were united as Great Britain under Queen Anne in 1707.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_British_monarchs" title="List of British monarchs">List of British monarchs</a></div>
<p>After Anne's accession, the problem of the succession re-emerged. The Scottish Parliament, infuriated that the English Parliament did not consult them on the choice of Sophia's family as the next heirs, passed the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Security_1704" title="Act of Security 1704">Act of Security 1704</a>, threatening to end the personal union between England and Scotland. The Parliament of England retaliated with the <a href="/wiki/Alien_Act_1705" title="Alien Act 1705">Alien Act 1705</a>, threatening to devastate the Scottish economy by restricting trade. The Scottish and English parliaments negotiated the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1707" title="Acts of Union 1707">Acts of Union 1707</a>, under which England and Scotland were united into a single <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Kingdom of Great Britain</a>, with succession under the rules prescribed by the Act of Settlement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1714, Queen Anne was succeeded by her second cousin, and Sophia's son, <a href="/wiki/George_I_of_Great_Britain" title="George I of Great Britain">George I</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elector_of_Hanover" title="Elector of Hanover">Elector of Hanover</a>, who consolidated his position by defeating Jacobite rebellions in 1715 and 1719. The new monarch was less active in government than many of his British predecessors, but retained control over his German kingdoms, with which Britain was now in personal union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Power shifted towards George's ministers, especially to Sir <a href="/wiki/Robert_Walpole" title="Robert Walpole">Robert Walpole</a>, who is often considered the first <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">British prime minister</a>, although the title was not then in use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> The next monarch, <a href="/wiki/George_II_of_Great_Britain" title="George II of Great Britain">George II</a>, witnessed the final end of the Jacobite threat in 1746, when the Catholic Stuarts were completely defeated. During the long reign of his grandson, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_III" title="George III">George III</a>, Britain's American colonies were lost, the former colonies having formed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_of_America" title="United States of America">United States of America</a>, but British influence elsewhere in the world continued to grow, and the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland</a> was created by the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1800" title="Acts of Union 1800">Acts of Union 1800</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom-e.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1393" data-file-width="961" height="319" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom-e.jpg/220px-George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom-e.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom-e.jpg/330px-George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom-e.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom-e.jpg/440px-George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom-e.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom-e.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The union of Great Britain and Ireland into the United Kingdom occurred in 1801 during the reign of King George III.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From 1811 to 1820, George III suffered a severe bout of what is now believed to be <a href="/wiki/Porphyria" title="Porphyria">porphyria</a>, an illness rendering him incapable of ruling. His son, the future <a href="/wiki/George_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George IV of the United Kingdom">George IV</a>, ruled in his stead as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regent_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Regent of the United Kingdom">Prince Regent</a>. During <a href="/wiki/Regency_era" title="Regency era">the Regency</a> and his own reign, the power of the monarchy declined, and by the time of his successor, <a href="/wiki/William_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="William IV of the United Kingdom">William IV</a>, the monarch was no longer able to effectively interfere with parliamentary power. In 1834, William dismissed the Whig Prime Minister, <a href="/wiki/William_Lamb,_2nd_Viscount_Melbourne" title="William Lamb, 2nd Viscount Melbourne">William Lamb, 2nd Viscount Melbourne</a>, and appointed a Tory, Sir <a href="/wiki/Robert_Peel" title="Robert Peel">Robert Peel</a>. In the ensuing elections, however, Peel lost. The king had no choice but to recall Lord Melbourne. During William IV's reign, the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1832" title="Reform Act 1832">Reform Act 1832</a>, which reformed parliamentary representation, was passed. Together with others passed later in the century, the Act led to an expansion of the electoral franchise and the rise of the House of Commons as the most important branch of Parliament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>The final transition to a <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a> was made during the long reign of William IV's successor, <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Victoria</a>. As a woman, Victoria could not rule <a href="/wiki/Hanover" title="Hanover">Hanover</a>, which only permitted succession in the male line, so the personal union of the United Kingdom and Hanover came to an end. The <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a> was marked by great cultural change, technological progress, and the establishment of the United Kingdom as one of the world's foremost powers. In recognition of British rule over India, Victoria was declared <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empress_of_India" title="Empress of India">Empress of India</a> in 1876. However, her reign was also marked by increased support for the <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Republicanism in the United Kingdom">republican movement</a>, due in part to Victoria's permanent mourning and lengthy period of seclusion following the death of <a href="/wiki/Albert,_Prince_Consort" title="Albert, Prince Consort">her husband</a> in 1861.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>Victoria's son, <a href="/wiki/Edward_VII" title="Edward VII">Edward VII</a>, became the first monarch of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Saxe-Coburg_and_Gotha" title="House of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha">House of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha</a> in 1901. In 1917, the next monarch, <a href="/wiki/George_V" title="George V">George V</a>, changed "Saxe-Coburg and Gotha" to "Windsor" in response to the anti-German sympathies aroused by the <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">First World War</a>. George V's reign was marked by the separation of Ireland into Northern Ireland, which remained a part of the United Kingdom, and the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Free_State" title="Irish Free State">Irish Free State</a>, an independent nation, in 1922.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shared_monarchy">Shared monarchy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Shared monarchy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:British_Empire_1921.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="625" data-file-width="1425" height="96" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/British_Empire_1921.png/220px-British_Empire_1921.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/British_Empire_1921.png/330px-British_Empire_1921.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/British_Empire_1921.png/440px-British_Empire_1921.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:British_Empire_1921.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of the British Empire in 1921</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Commonwealth_Realms_map2.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="628" data-file-width="1357" height="102" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Commonwealth_Realms_map2.png/220px-Commonwealth_Realms_map2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Commonwealth_Realms_map2.png/330px-Commonwealth_Realms_map2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Commonwealth_Realms_map2.png/440px-Commonwealth_Realms_map2.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Commonwealth_Realms_map2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of the Commonwealth realms today</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the twentieth century, the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a> evolved from the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>. Prior to 1926, the British Crown reigned over the British Empire collectively; the <a href="/wiki/Dominion" title="Dominion">Dominions</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crown_Colonies" title="Crown Colonies">Crown Colonies</a> were subordinate to the United Kingdom. The <a href="/wiki/Balfour_Declaration_of_1926" title="Balfour Declaration of 1926">Balfour Declaration of 1926</a> gave complete self-government to the Dominions, effectively creating a system whereby a single monarch operated independently in each separate Dominion. The concept was solidified by the <a href="/wiki/Statute_of_Westminster_1931" title="Statute of Westminster 1931">Statute of Westminster 1931</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> which has been likened to "a treaty among the Commonwealth countries".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>The monarchy thus ceased to be an exclusively British institution, although it is often still referred to as "British" for legal and historical reasons and for convenience. The monarch became separately monarch of the United Kingdom, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monarch_of_Canada" title="Monarch of Canada">monarch of Canada</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monarch_of_Australia" title="Monarch of Australia">monarch of Australia</a>, and so forth. The independent states within the Commonwealth would share the same monarch in a relationship likened to a <a href="/wiki/Personal_union" title="Personal union">personal union</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>George V's death in 1936 was followed by the accession of <a href="/wiki/Edward_VIII" title="Edward VIII">Edward VIII</a>, who caused a public scandal by announcing his desire to marry the divorced American <a href="/wiki/Wallis_Simpson" title="Wallis Simpson">Wallis Simpson</a>, even though the Church of England opposed the remarriage of divorcées. Accordingly, Edward announced his intention to <a href="/wiki/Edward_VIII_abdication_crisis" title="Edward VIII abdication crisis">abdicate</a>; the Parliaments of the United Kingdom and of other Commonwealth countries granted his request. Edward VIII and any children by his new wife were excluded from the line of succession, and the Crown went to his brother, <a href="/wiki/George_VI" title="George VI">George VI</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> George served as a rallying figure for the British people during World War II, making morale-boosting visits to the troops as well as to munitions factories and to areas bombed by <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>. In June 1948 George VI relinquished the title <i>Emperor of India</i>, although remaining head of state of the <a href="/wiki/Dominion_of_India" title="Dominion of India">Dominion of India</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>At first, every member of the Commonwealth retained the same monarch as the United Kingdom, but when the Dominion of India became a republic in 1950, it would no longer share in a common monarchy. Instead, the British monarch was acknowledged as "<a href="/wiki/Head_of_the_Commonwealth" title="Head of the Commonwealth">Head of the Commonwealth</a>" in all Commonwealth member states, whether they were realms or republics. The position is purely ceremonial, and is not inherited by the British monarch as of right but is vested in an individual chosen by the Commonwealth heads of government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hoc_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-hoc-72">[72]</a></sup> Member states of the Commonwealth that share the same person as monarch are known as <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_realm" title="Commonwealth realm">Commonwealth realms</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monarchy_in_Ireland">Monarchy in Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Monarchy in Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_Ireland" title="Monarchy of Ireland">Monarchy of Ireland</a></div>
<p>In 1155 the only English pope, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adrian_IV" title="Adrian IV">Adrian IV</a>, authorised King <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">Henry II of England</a> to take possession of Ireland as a feudal territory nominally under papal overlordship. The pope wanted the English monarch to annex Ireland and bring the Irish church into line with Rome, despite this process already underway in Ireland by 1155.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> An all-island kingship of Ireland had been created in 854 by <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A1el_Sechnaill_mac_M%C3%A1ele_Ruanaid" title="Máel Sechnaill mac Máele Ruanaid">Máel Sechnaill mac Máele Ruanaid</a>. His last successor was <a href="/wiki/Ruaidr%C3%AD_Ua_Conchobair" title="Ruaidrí Ua Conchobair">Ruaidrí Ua Conchobair</a>, who had become King of Ireland in early 1166, and exiled <a href="/wiki/Diarmait_Mac_Murchada" title="Diarmait Mac Murchada">Diarmait Mac Murchada</a>, King of Leinster. Diarmait asked Henry II for help, gaining a group of Anglo-Norman aristocrats and adventurers, led by <a href="/wiki/Richard_de_Clare,_2nd_Earl_of_Pembroke" title="Richard de Clare, 2nd Earl of Pembroke">Richard de Clare, 2nd Earl of Pembroke</a>, to help him regain his throne. Diarmait and his Anglo-Norman allies succeeded and he became King of Leinster again. De Clare married Diarmait's daughter, and when Diarmait died in 1171, de Clare became King of Leinster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Henry was afraid that de Clare would make Ireland a rival Norman kingdom, so he took advantage of the papal bull and invaded, forcing de Clare and the other Anglo-Norman aristocrats in Ireland and the major Irish kings and lords to recognise him as their <a href="/wiki/Lordship_of_Ireland" title="Lordship of Ireland">overlord</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> English lords came close to colonising the entire island, but a Gaelic resurgence from the 1260s resulted in the island divided between Gaelic-Irish and Anglo-Irish lords by 1400. Many of the latter became completely Gaelicised, and did not recognise England's kings except perhaps nominally. Some, such as <a href="/wiki/Manus_O%27Donnell" title="Manus O'Donnell">Manus O'Donnell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Conn_O%27Neill,_1st_Earl_of_Tyrone" title="Conn O'Neill, 1st Earl of Tyrone">Conn O'Neill, 1st Earl of Tyrone</a>, were kings themselves.</p>
<p>By 1541, King Henry VIII of England had broken with the Church of Rome and declared himself <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Head_of_the_Church_of_England" title="Supreme Head of the Church of England">Supreme Head of the Church of England</a>. The pope's grant of Ireland to the English monarch became invalid, so Henry summoned a meeting of the Irish Parliament to change his title from Lord of Ireland to King of Ireland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> However much of the island was beyond English control, resulting in the extended <a href="/wiki/Tudor_conquest_of_Ireland" title="Tudor conquest of Ireland">Tudor conquest of Ireland</a> that only made the Kingdom of Ireland a reality in 1603, at the conclusion of the <a href="/wiki/Nine_Years%27_War_(Ireland)" title="Nine Years' War (Ireland)">Nine Years' War (Ireland)</a>.
